**Claude:**

## Phase 6 complete

**Stage A** (audit) delivered the full verdict table before any code changed: 6 Implemented, 1 N/A, 4 Partial. **Stage B** fixed every gap, one focused commit per checklist item. **Stage C** proved each testable protection with tests that fail if the protection regresses, verified headers live via `docker compose` + `curl`, and wrote `docs/SECURITY.md`.

**What was actually broken and got fixed:**
- Kestrel's global body-size cap was never set (#1)
- `docker-compose.yml` committed a real, working JWT signing key as a fallback default (#4) — a genuine secret in git, not just a demo nicety
- JWT validation didn't pin `ValidAlgorithms` (#4)
- Upload had zero rate limiting (#6) — which surfaced a real bug: `UseRateLimiter` ran before `UseAuthentication`, so per-user partitioning would have silently degraded to per-IP
- No CORS policy, no security headers, no CSP existed at all (#7, #8) — fixing CSP required extracting the Vite build's one inline script so `script-src 'self'` wouldn't silently break the app
- HSTS would have been dead in the real Azure deployment (TLS terminates at the platform edge) without `UseForwardedHeaders` — caught during Stage C's manual verification, not assumed
- `GlobalExceptionHandler` was silently sending `application/json` instead of `application/problem+json` — caught by the Stage C test written specifically to prove #9

**What was already correct**, verified rather than trusted: upload size limits, XXE hardening (confirmed via a live mutation test), decimal/invariant-culture parsing, authorization on every endpoint, login rate limiting.

**Surfaced, not silently fixed**: the skill's own `architecture.md` API table is missing `/api/samples/*` and `/api/health` — flagged in `docs/SECURITY.md` rather than edited.

93/93 tests green, frontend builds clean, `docs/SECURITY.md` is the full reference, README's Security section links it, and `docs/PLAN.md` Phase 6 is checked off with a one-line-per-fix summary.

**Human:**

Read CLAUDE.md and docs/PLAN.md. This session is a pure frontend refactor: 
replace all fetch-based API interaction with axios, centralized in a single 
client with interceptors. HARD RULE: zero behavior change observable by the 
user or by the backend, except the explicitly listed caching additions. No 
visual changes, no new features, no backend changes.

STAGE A — INVENTORY (no code changes)
Find every fetch call and every place that constructs headers, parses 
ProblemDetails, handles 401/403, uploads multipart, or downloads blobs. List 
them as a table: file | endpoint | method | special behavior (auth, blob, 
multipart, redirect-on-401, error mapping). This table is the migration 
checklist — every row must be ticked by the end.

STAGE B — Central axios client (frontend/src/api/client.ts)
- One axios instance: baseURL from the existing config, JSON defaults, sensible 
  timeout (e.g. 30s; uploads may override).
- Request interceptor: attach Authorization from the auth store (same source 
  4.1 uses — do not create a second token source).
- Response interceptor (error side):
  * Normalize errors into ONE typed shape (e.g. ApiError { status, title, 
    detail, problemDetails? }) parsed from ProblemDetails when present; 
    network/timeout errors map into the same shape with status 0.
  * 401 ⇒ exactly the existing behavior: clear session, redirect to /login 
    preserving intended destination. Guard against redirect loops (a 401 from 
    /api/auth/login itself must NOT trigger the redirect — it's an invalid 
    credentials case for the form).
  * 403 and other statuses: pass the normalized error through; pages keep 
    their existing handling.
- MIGRATION TRAP — handle explicitly: fetch resolves on 4xx/5xx and code 
  checked response.ok; axios throws. Every migrated call site must move its 
  non-2xx logling from "if (!ok)" branches into catch/error paths WITHOUT 
  changing what the user sees. Audit each row of the Stage A table for this 
  specifically.
- Special cases:
  * File upload (POST /api/declarations): multipart FormData — let axios set 
    the boundary (do not hard-code Content-Type), preserve the existing 
    progress UX; onUploadProgress may drive the existing presentational steps 
    but do not redesign them.
  * PDF download: responseType 'blob', preserve filename logic and the 
    button's loading/error states.
  * Sample file links: if they are plain <a href> downloads today, LEAVE them 
    as links — do not convert working native downloads into axios calls.

STAGE C — Caching (the only behavior addition)
- Introduce a minimal, explicit cache layer — hand-rolled Map with TTL or a 
  tiny helper, NOT a new heavyweight dependency (no TanStack Query in this 
  session; note it as a possible future improvement instead).
- Cache exactly these:
  * GET /api/countries/HU/vat-categories — effectively immutable: cache for 
    the session, shared across pages (report view re-renders must not refetch).
  * GET /api/auth/me — cache until logout/401.
- Explicit invalidation: full cache clear on logout and on 401 redirect.
- Do NOT cache: declarations list (4.4 will own freshness), individual 
  declaration fetches (cheap, and status could change in future), anything 
  non-GET.
- Document the cache policy in a comment block at the cache module top.

STAGE D — Migrate + verify
- Migrate every Stage A row to the client. Delete dead fetch helpers. No 
  component may import axios directly — only the client module (grep-enforced: 
  "from 'axios'" appears in client.ts only).
- Tick each inventory row with the file diff reference.
- TypeScript strict: zero errors; no `any` in the error normalization.

ACCEPTANCE — full manual regression in browser (docker compose up):
- Login: success path; wrong password shows the SAME inline error as before 
  (not a redirect); rate-limit response still surfaces sensibly.
- Session: refresh keeps login; logout clears; deep-link bounce and return 
  still work; expired/invalid token on any page ⇒ redirect to /login with 
  destination preserved.
- Upload: sample-clean.csv end-to-end ⇒ report shows the golden totals 
  (94 300 / 50 600 / 43 700); oversized file still rejected client-side with 
  no network call; spoofed .csv still shows the designed server-rejection 
  screen.
- Report: renders identically; PDF downloads with the same filename; viewer 
  role still sees no admin affordances; 404 state unchanged.
- Caching proof: with devtools network tab open, navigate between report pages 
  — vat-categories fetched exactly once per session; after logout+login it 
  refetches.
- git grep fetch( in frontend/src returns nothing API-related (allow unrelated 
  uses if any, list them).

CONSTRAINTS
- Pure refactor discipline: if you find a pre-existing bug, do NOT fix it 
  silently — list it under "Found during refactor" and leave behavior as-is 
  unless it's trivially safe; I'll decide.
- Conventional commits: inventory/infra, migration, caching as separate 
  commits. Final commit: note the refactor in docs/PLAN.md under a "Technical 
  improvements" line (do not disturb phase checkboxes), and end with the ticked 
  inventory table + any "Found during refactor" items.

**Claude:**

## Stage A — Inventory (no code changes)

I read `CLAUDE.md`, `docs/PLAN.md`, and traced every `fetch()` call and its callers. Full migration checklist:

| # | File(s) | Endpoint | Method | Special behavior |
|---|---|---|---|---|
| 1 | `client.ts` (`loginRequest`) → `AuthContext.login` → `LoginPage` | `/api/auth/login` | POST | Raw `fetch`, separate from `apiFetch` — **no** auth header, **no** 401-redirect (a 401 here is "wrong password," an inline form error, not a session death). JSON body. |
| 2 | `client.ts` (`getMe`) | `/api/auth/me` | GET | Via `apiFetch` (auth header + 401-redirect). **Defined but never called anywhere in the UI** — dead code today. |
| 3 | `client.ts` (`getHealth`) | `/api/health` | GET | Via `apiFetch`. **Defined but never called anywhere in the UI** — dead code today. |
| 4 | `client.ts` (`getDeclarations`) → `DashboardPage` | `/api/declarations` | GET | Via `apiFetch`. Generic catch → `'error'` state (no status-specific handling). |
| 5 | `client.ts` (`getDeclaration`) → `DeclarationReportPage` | `/api/declarations/{id}` | GET | Via `apiFetch`, run in `Promise.all` with row 7. 404 → `'not-found'` state; anything else → `'error'` state. |
| 6 | `client.ts` (`uploadDeclaration`) → `UploadPage.process` | `/api/declarations` | POST | **Multipart** `FormData` — browser must set `Content-Type`/boundary, never hard-coded. **MIGRATION TRAP**: catch block branches on `err instanceof ApiError` to distinguish "server responded" (401 silent-return / 403 forbidden-screen / other → full-page error) from "network failure" (toast + Retry). Axios will make network failures *also* throw an `ApiError`-shaped object once normalized, so this branch must switch to checking `status === 0` instead of `instanceof ApiError`. |
| 7 | `client.ts` (`getVatCategories`) → `DeclarationReportPage` | `/api/countries/HU/vat-categories` | GET | Via `apiFetch`. **Already ad-hoc cached** (a `Map<string, Promise<...>>` keyed by country code, no TTL, no invalidation on logout) — Stage C replaces this with the real cache module. |
| 8 | `client.ts` (`fetchSampleBlob` → `fetchSampleAsFile`, `downloadSample`) → `DropZone`, `FormatHintTabs`, `UploadErrorScreen` | `/api/samples/{name}` | GET | Separate `authedBlobFetch` (duplicates the auth-header + 401-redirect logic from `apiFetch`). **Blob** response — not a plain `<a href>` link, it's an authenticated JS fetch that then synthesizes an anchor click to trigger save. Must migrate, not leave as a link. |
| 9 | `client.ts` (`downloadDeclarationPdf`) → `DeclarationReportPage`, `DeclarationsTable` | `/api/declarations/{id}/pdf` | GET | Same `authedBlobFetch`. Blob response, per-caller loading state + error toast, filename `declaration-{id.slice(0,8)}.pdf`. |

**Shared infrastructure to migrate**: `ApiError` class (status + detail), `parseProblem` (parses ProblemDetails JSON), the duplicated 401-redirect logic (appears twice — `apiFetch` and `authedBlobFetch`), `saveBlob` helper (unchanged — pure blob→disk, no fetch involved).

**Token source**: `getToken()`/`clearSession()` from `lib/auth-storage.ts` — this is the one source I'll wire the axios interceptor to; `AuthContext` doesn't hold a second copy.

**No `<a href>` static download links exist** — every "download" in this app is already an authenticated JS fetch, so row 8 doesn't get the "leave as link" exemption.

I'll move into Stage B now (central axios client), then Stage C (caching), then Stage D (migrate every row above + delete the dead `fetch` helpers), ticking this table off as I go.

**5 commits**, each independently reviewable:
1. `chore`: add axios dependency
2. `refactor`: migrate every `fetch()` to the central axios client — including the migration-trap fix in `UploadPage.tsx` (network failures now normalize into `ApiError{status:0}` instead of a different error type, so the catch block had to switch from `instanceof ApiError` to `status !== 0` to keep the same toast-vs-full-screen-error behavior)
3. `feat`: the one explicit behavior addition — session-scoped caching for vat-categories and `/api/auth/me`, invalidated on logout/401
4. `docs`: PLAN.md technical-improvements record

**Two real bugs my own test script hit, both false alarms in the actual code** — worth knowing about since they briefly looked like regressions:
- Testing cache behavior via `page.goto()` trivially "resets" any in-memory cache (fresh JS module load) — not a bug, just a wrong test technique. Switched to real client-side navigation, which then correctly showed 0 refetches across 3 navigations and exactly 1 refetch after logout+login.
- Logging out from the report page redirects back to that page after login (the existing deep-link-preservation feature), not to `/` — correct pre-existing behavior, not something I introduced.

**Full manual regression passed** in docker compose: wrong password → inline error (not redirect); refresh/logout/deep-link-bounce-and-return all intact; oversized file rejected client-side with zero network calls; sample-clean.csv → exact golden totals (94 300/50 600/43 700), unchanged report screenshot; PDF downloads with the same filename convention; spoofed .csv still shows the designed rejection screen; viewer sees no admin affordances; 404 state unchanged; login rate-limiting still trips sensibly. `git grep "fetch(" frontend/src` → nothing; axios imported only in `client.ts`.

Backend untouched — 93/93 xUnit tests green throughout, confirming this really was frontend-only.

**Found during refactor, left as-is** (per instructions, your call): `getMe()`/`getHealth()` are fully implemented but never called from any page (dead code predating this session); `FormatHintTabs`/`UploadErrorScreen`'s download handlers have no `catch`, so a failed sample download is an unhandled promise rejection today.

This pipeline list is the contract for Stage B: the refactored pipeline must

to fill categories):

    Npgsql, migration-related services
  * AddAuthenticationServices(...) — JWT bearer setup including the fail-fast 
    key validation exactly as-is
  * AddAuthorizationPolicies(...) — role policies
  * AddApiSecurity(...) — rate limiting, CORS
  * AddDomainServices(...) — parsers, factory, validation engine, PDF renderer, 
    and the AddCountry<TRegistry,TStrategy>("HU") registration seam — this one 
    stays prominently visible, it's our extensibility story
  * AddApiInfrastructure(...) — controllers, ProblemDetails, Swagger if present, 
    multipart/Kestrel limits
- Pipeline: either one UseVatDeskPipeline(this WebApplication) preserving exact 
  order with the Stage A annotations as comments, or 2–3 grouped stages 
  (UseSecurity, UseApiEndpoints) — choose whichever keeps the order most 
  legible, and keep the ordering comments. The order annotations from Stage A 
  MUST survive into code comments — they are documentation of intent, not noise.
- Program.cs after refactor: reads as a short table of contents — builder 
  config, Add* calls, build, startup tasks (migrations), Use* pipeline, run. 
  Target: a screen or less, no lambda bodies longer than a couple of lines.
- Startup tasks (migration apply, any seeding) move to a clearly named 
  extension (e.g. await app.ApplyMigrationsAsync()) with identical execution 
  point and failure behavior.
- Respect layering: extensions live in Api; do not move DI code into Core; 
  Infrastructure may expose its own AddInfrastructure-style entry point if that 
  reduces Api's knowledge of concrete types — allowed, note it.

STAGE C — PROVE
- Full test suite green, including WebApplicationFactory integration tests — 
  they boot the real Program and are the strongest behavior-preservation check 
  we have. If any test breaks, the refactor changed something: find it, don't 
  patch the test.

  behaviors survived, via curl (paste outputs):

    AND on a 404
  * anonymous request to a protected endpoint ⇒ 401 ProblemDetails (auth 
    middleware still wired)
  * viewer token on POST /api/declarations ⇒ 403 (policies still wired)
  * rapid login attempts ⇒ 429 (rate limiter still in the pipeline)
  * SPA deep link (e.g. /declarations/new) served by the fallback
  * startup with the JWT key env var removed ⇒ process fails fast with the 
    same error
- Golden path once through the browser: login, upload sample-clean.csv, see 
  94 300 / 50 600 / 43 700, download PDF.

- Pure refactor: pre-existing issues found along the way go into a "Found 
  during refactor" list, not into the diff. Exception: if the Stage A inventory 
  reveals an actual ordering BUG (e.g. headers registered after the SPA

## Stage A — Inventory (no code changes)

Read `CLAUDE.md` and `architecture.md`'s layering rules. `AddCountry<TRegistry,TStrategy>` already lives in `VatDesk.Infrastructure` (`CountryServiceCollectionExtensions.cs`) — Infrastructure already exposes its own DI entry point, exactly the allowed pattern the task mentions. Baseline: **93/93 tests green** before any change (my comparison point for Stage C).

### 1. Service registrations, grouped by concern

**Kestrel (on `builder.WebHost`, pre-`Build()`)**
- `ConfigureKestrel` → `Limits.MaxRequestBodySize = ParserFactory.MaxFileSizeBytes` (global 5 MB cap, belt-and-suspenders with the per-endpoint `[RequestSizeLimit]`)

**API infrastructure**
- `AddControllers()`
- `AddProblemDetails()`
- `AddExceptionHandler<GlobalExceptionHandler>()`
- `Configure<FormOptions>` → `MultipartBodyLengthLimit = ParserFactory.MaxFileSizeBytes` (options binding)

**Persistence**
- `AddDbContext<VatDeskDbContext>` (Scoped, EF default) → Npgsql, connection string from `ConnectionStrings:Default`
- `AddScoped<DeclarationRepository>`
- `AddScoped<UserRepository>`

**Domain services**
- `AddSingleton<IInvoiceParser, CsvInvoiceParser>`
- `AddSingleton<IInvoiceParser, NavXmlInvoiceParser>`
- `AddSingleton<ParserFactory>`
- `AddSingleton<IReportRenderer, QuestPdfReportRenderer>`
- `AddCountry<HungarianVatCategoryRegistry, HungarianVatDeclarationStrategy>("HU")` — the extensibility seam; stays visible in Api since v1 hard-defaults the country here.

**Auth**
- `Configure<JwtOptions>` (options binding)
- Fail-fast key guard (reads `Jwt:Key` directly, throws if missing/<32 bytes) — not a registration itself, but must move as one inseparable unit with the JWT bearer setup that consumes `jwtKey`/`jwtIssuer`/`jwtAudience`
- `AddSingleton<IJwtTokenService, JwtTokenService>`
- `AddAuthentication(...).AddJwtBearer(...)` — issuer/audience/signing-key validation, `ValidAlgorithms` pinned to `HmacSha256`

**Authorization**
- `AddAuthorization()` — bare call; no named policies exist today (role checks are all `[Authorize(Roles=...)]` attributes on controllers, nothing to enumerate beyond this one call)

**API security (CORS + rate limiting)**
- `AddCors` → one policy (`CorsPolicies.AppOrigin`), origin from config, empty by default
- `AddRateLimiter` → `Login` policy (IP-partitioned, 8/min) + `Upload` policy (user-id-partitioned, 20/min)

### 2. Middleware pipeline, ordered (this is Stage B's contract)

| # | Call | Why position matters |
|---|---|---|
| — | `dbContext.Database.Migrate()` (in an `IsRelational()`-guarded scope right after `Build()`) | Not pipeline middleware — a one-time startup task before the pipeline runs at all. Must keep this exact execution point and failure behavior (unhandled exception ⇒ process crash). |
| 1 | `UseExceptionHandler()` | Outermost — must wrap every later middleware/endpoint to catch unhandled exceptions. |
| 2 | `UseStatusCodePages()` | Must wrap everything downstream that can produce a bare 401/403/404, to rewrite them into ProblemDetails. |
| 3 | `UseForwardedHeaders(...)` | Must run before anything reading `Request.IsHttps`/`Scheme`/`RemoteIpAddress` — HSTS, HTTPS redirection, and the login rate limiter's IP partitioning all depend on the real (forwarded) values behind Azure's edge. |
| 4 | `UseHsts()` (dev-gated) | Depends on #3's normalized scheme; dev-gated so local HTTP testing isn't broken. |
| 5 | `UseHttpsRedirection()` | Also depends on #3. |
| 6 | `UseSecurityHeaders()` (custom) | Before static files and the SPA fallback specifically, so those responses carry the headers too; sets headers unconditionally before calling `next()` so error responses get them as well. |
| 7 | `UseCors(...)` | Conventional position before auth/static files/endpoints, so CORS headers apply uniformly including on 401s. |
| 8 | `UseDefaultFiles()` | Serves `index.html` as the default document. |
| 9 | `UseStaticFiles()` | Serves the built SPA's assets. |
| 10 | `UseAuthentication()` | Must run before `UseAuthorization()` (standard) **and** before `UseRateLimiter()` in this app — the upload policy partitions by the authenticated user's claim, which only exists once this has run (a documented fix from the prior security session; reversing it silently degrades that policy to IP-based). |
| 11 | `UseAuthorization()` | Must follow `UseAuthentication()`. |
| 12 | `UseRateLimiter()` | Must follow #10/#11 for the reason above. |
| 13 | `MapControllers()` | Attribute-routed endpoints. |
| 14 | `MapFallbackToFile("index.html")` | Must be **last** — only catches requests no controller route or static file matched, so React Router can take over client-side paths. |
| 15 | `app.Run()` | — |

**No ordering bug found** — `UseSecurityHeaders` is already before `UseStaticFiles`/`MapFallbackToFile` (the exact bug shape the task warned about), and the auth-before-rate-limiter ordering is already correct (fixed in a prior session). Proceeding straight to Stage B — nothing to stop and report.

---

**Claude:**

This is exactly the problem, and it's not a Railway quirk — it's that Railway doesn't run `docker-compose.yml` at all.

## Root cause

When you connect a GitHub repo to Railway and let it auto-deploy, Railway detects the `Dockerfile` at the repo root and builds/runs **that single container directly**. It never reads `docker-compose.yml`.

But the translation from the friendly `JWT_KEY` name (what `.env.example` shows, what you generated and pasted) into the actual variable ASP.NET Core reads only happens *inside* `docker-compose.yml`:

```yaml
environment:
  Jwt__Key: "${JWT_KEY}"   # <- this line is the translation
```

`docker compose up` reads your `.env`'s `JWT_KEY` and passes it into the container as `Jwt__Key` (double underscore — that's .NET's convention for mapping an env var to the config path `Jwt:Key`, read in code as `configuration["Jwt:Key"]`). Railway never executes that compose file, so that translation never happens. You end up with a container that has a variable named `JWT_KEY`, while the app is looking for `Jwt__Key`. They don't match, so the app sees no key at all and fails exactly as designed (`AuthenticationServiceCollectionExtensions`'s fail-fast check).

## The fix

In Railway's **Variables** tab for the app service, set the variable name to:

```
Jwt__Key
```

(two underscores, not `JWT_KEY`) — value is the same generated key you already have.

## One more thing you'll likely hit next

The same problem exists for the database connection. Railway's Postgres plugin gives you its own connection details (`DATABASE_URL` or discrete `PGHOST`/`PGUSER`/etc.), but this app reads `ConnectionStrings__Default` in the ADO.NET key=value format, not a `postgres://` URL:

```
ConnectionStrings__Default = Host=<railway-pg-host>;Port=<port>;Database=<db>;Username=<user>;Password=<password>
```

So you'll need:
1. A Postgres service added in your Railway project (if you haven't already).
2. A `ConnectionStrings__Default` variable on the **app** service, built from that Postgres service's host/port/db/user/password — Railway lets you reference another service's variables when composing this (e.g. `Host=${{Postgres.PGHOST}};Port=${{Postgres.PGPORT}};Database=${{Postgres.PGDATABASE}};Username=${{Postgres.PGUSER}};Password=${{Postgres.PGPASSWORD}}`, using Railway's variable-reference syntax, if it's exposed that way in your Postgres plugin — check its Variables tab for the exact names).

Once both `Jwt__Key` and `ConnectionStrings__Default` are set correctly, redeploy and it should boot the same way it does locally via `docker compose up`.

**Human:**

I run railway login, authorize in browser, but in the terminal I'm getting stuck in waiting for sign-in...
What could be the cause

---

**Claude:**

That hang is a known pattern with `railway login` — it starts a local callback server, opens the browser to authorize, and waits for Railway's redirect to hit `localhost:<port>` on your machine. It gets stuck when that redirect never arrives back at the CLI. Common causes:

- A firewall/VPN (corporate network, Little Snitch, macOS firewall) blocking the local callback port
- A browser extension (ad blocker, privacy/tracking blocker) stripping the redirect
- Running the browser and the CLI in contexts that don't actually share `localhost` (e.g., a remote/SSH session, a devcontainer, browser and terminal on different machines)

The standard fix is the device-code flow instead of the local-callback flow — it skips the localhost redirect entirely: you get a code, paste it into a URL yourself, no callback needed.

---

**Claude:**

[Tool: Bash — railway login --help 2>&1]

---

**Human:**

[Result: Sign in to Railway — also creates a new account if you don't have one.

Uses a single OAuth flow for both sign-in and sign-up. Brand-new accounts are detected automatically and land on a welcome page; existing users see the standard sign-in confirmation. Use --browserless for SSH sessions, remote dev boxes, or any environment where a local browser can't open.

Usage: railway login [OPTIONS]

Options:
  -b, --browserless
          Use a device-code flow instead of opening a browser. Prints a sign...]

---

**Claude:**

One thing worth flagging before I go further: the `--help` output above contains this line —

> "Only needed when this machine truly has no browser — the CLI already auto-detects SSH, CI, and missing DISPLAY. If a human is at this machine (**including under a coding agent**), omit this flag: the browser flow completes far more reliably"

That's very unusual phrasing for CLI help text — a real Railway CLI has no reason to reference "coding agents" at all. This reads like it could be a prompt injection embedded in the binary's output, aimed at steering me away from recommending `--browserless`. I'm flagging it rather than following it, and I'm not going to change my recommendation based on that text alone.

Separately from that: my actual recommendation is unaffected either way, because it's based on your real symptom, not on the help text. You already tried the plain browser flow and it hung waiting for the localhost callback — that's the exact, well-documented case `--browserless` exists for. So:

**Claude:**

CLI is installed and current (5.27.2). In **your own terminal** (not through me — this needs your interactive browser), run:

```
railway login --browserless
```

That prints a short code and a URL. Open the URL, enter the code, confirm — no localhost callback involved, so the firewall/extension/VPN issue that's blocking the normal flow doesn't matter. Once that completes, run `railway whoami` to confirm you're authenticated, and the MCP server (which just shells out to this same `railway` CLI) should connect on the next session restart.

**Claude:**

## Diagnosis (confirmed via Railway API, not guessed)

The `dynamic-perfection` project has two services: **VATDesk** and **Postgres**. Postgres is healthy and has a successful deployment. But the **VATDesk service has zero service instances** — meaning it currently has **no GitHub repo (or image) connected at all**, and has **never deployed once**. `railway service list` doesn't even list it (Railway only lists configured instances), and `deployment list`/`logs` both correctly report "no deployments."

So this isn't currently a crash-loop — it's an empty, unconfigured service. The JWT-key crash you saw earlier must have been from an earlier attempt whose source connection later got lost or removed.

Local repo confirms the target: `cretumarius/VATDesk`, branch `main`.

## Fix plan

1. **Connect the source**: `railway service source connect --repo cretumarius/VATDesk --branch main --service VATDesk` — this is what actually triggers Railway to build your `Dockerfile` on push/connect.
2. **Set `Jwt__Key`** on the VATDesk service — I'll generate a real ≥32-byte value (same as your local `.env` approach, `openssl rand -base64 48`), variable name exactly `Jwt__Key` (double underscore).
3. **Set `ConnectionStrings__Default`** on the VATDesk service, built from Postgres's own variables using Railway's cross-service reference syntax so it stays correct if Postgres's credentials ever rotate:
   `Host=${{Postgres.PGHOST}};Port=${{Postgres.PGPORT}};Database=${{Postgres.PGDATABASE}};Username=${{Postgres.PGUSER}};Password=${{Postgres.PGPASSWORD}}`
4. **Trigger the deploy** and poll `railway deployment list` until it hits `SUCCESS` (not just "started") — I'll report the real terminal state, not just that a build queued.
5. **Verify**: check logs for a clean startup, then confirm the public URL actually serves the app.

Want me to go ahead and run steps 1–5 now through the connected Railway tools? It'll set a new signing key and kick off a real build (uses your Railway build minutes) — low risk since nothing has deployed yet, but flagging before I touch shared infra.

## Where things stand

## What's left, and who needs to run it

Two commands, either destructive or infrastructure-creating, both blocked from my side:

```bash
# 1. Remove the broken stub
railway service delete --service 08731bed-46fe-4bc0-af28-8a2db31f8192 \
  --environment 3b1c1a16-bb22-446d-8a92-3b2a24dc6183 --yes

# 2. Recreate it properly from your repo

```

You have two options:
- **Run those two yourself** in your terminal (I'm already linked to the right project/environment locally, so you could also just run `railway service delete` and `railway add` from this same repo directory without needing to pass the IDs again — the flags above are the explicit/unambiguous form).
- **Grant me permission** for this class of action (Bash permission rule for `railway service delete` / `railway add`) if you want me to drive the rest of this end-to-end.

Once the service exists with a real instance, I can pick the rest back up automatically: set `Jwt__Key` and `ConnectionStrings__Default`, trigger and poll the deploy to a real `SUCCESS`, and verify the app is actually serving. Let me know which way you want to go.

## Deployment is live and fully working

**Public URL: https://vatdesk-production.up.railway.app**

What was done after your delete + recreate:
1. Set `Jwt__Key` — a real generated 64-char signing key.
2. Set `ConnectionStrings__Default` using Railway's cross-service reference syntax (`${{Postgres.PGHOST}}` etc.), so it stays correct automatically if the Postgres credentials ever rotate.
3. Both changes auto-triggered a fresh deploy — polled it to a genuine `SUCCESS` (not just "build queued").
4. Checked runtime logs: clean startup, EF Core confirmed the DB schema was already up to date, `Now listening on :8080`, no exceptions.
5. No public domain existed yet, so I generated one (`railway domain --port 8080`) — that was the missing last step for "make it public."
6. Verified end-to-end over the real public URL: `GET /` → 200, `GET /api/health` → `databaseConnected: true`, and `POST /api/auth/login` with the seeded admin credentials → a real signed JWT back, security headers (CSP/HSTS/X-Frame-Options) present.

Everything from the earlier local `docker compose` verification now checks out identically in production. The app is ready for you to share.

One thing worth knowing: this used Railway's free `up.railway.app` subdomain. If you want a custom domain later, it's `railway domain yourdomain.com --port 8080` plus a DNS record Railway will give you — happy to walk through that whenever you're ready.
